\DIFaddend \section{Electronics Design}

\DIFdelbegin \subsection{\DIFdel{Processing core unit}}
%DIFAUXCMD
\addtocounter{subsection}{-1}%DIFAUXCMD
\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{The overall electronics architecture of the \mbox{%DIFAUXCMD
\gls{mirage} }\hskip0pt%DIFAUXCMD
experiment is shown in \fref{fig:electrical_schematic}. The system consists of a Power Distribution Board, which interfaces with the BEXUS gondola power supply and provides regulated voltages to the experiment subsystems. The processing core is based on an Arduino Mega microcontroller, which manages sensor data acquisition, system control, and communication with onboard storage.
}\DIFaddend 

\DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{Additional subsystems include the pressurization system, the thermal control system, the K96 NDIR sensor core, and external environmental sensors measuring ambient temperature, pressure, and humidity. The ambient sensors communicate with the processing unit via an I²C interface, while the K96 NDIR sensor communicates using a UART interface. The interactions between these subsystems and the processing unit are illustrated in \fref{fig:electrical_schematic}. Each subsystem is described in more detail in the following sections.
}

\DIFaddend \begin{figure}[H]
    \centering

                \DIFdelbeginFL %DIFDELCMD < \label{fig:component_arduino}
%DIFDELCMD < %%%

                \DIFaddendFL \end{figure}

\DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{An Arduino mega }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \subsection{\DIFadd{Processing core unit}}

%DIF >     \label{fig:component_arduino}
%DIF > \end{figure}


\DIFadd{An Arduino Mega }\DIFaddend will be used for preliminary testing and \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{is to }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{as a backup solution, and will }\DIFaddend be replaced by a \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{processing \mbox{%DIFAUXCMD
\ac{pcb} }\hskip0pt%DIFAUXCMD
once assembly starts}\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{dedicated processing \mbox{%DIFAUXCMD

The Arduino \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{mega }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{Mega }\DIFaddend is equipped with an \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{ethernet }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{Ethernet }\DIFaddend shield, enabling it to communicate with EGSE and save measurement data to an \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{SD-card}\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{SD card}\DIFaddend . The Arduino needs a power input between 7-12V\DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{, }\DIFaddend which will be converted from the 28.8V \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{from the SAFT batteries }\DIFdelend provided by Bexus\DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{' SAFT batteries }\DIFaddend through a DC/DC stepdown regulator.

\subsection{\DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{Power Distribution Unit}\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{MicroSD Card}\DIFaddend }

\DIFdelbegin %DIFDELCMD < \begin{figure}[H]
%DIFDELCMD <     \centering
%DIFDELCMD <     \includegraphics[width=0.3\textwidth]{Graphics/Figures/Stepdown DCDC.png}
%DIFDELCMD <     %%%
%DIFDELCMD < \caption{%
{%DIFAUXCMD
\DIFdelFL{LM2596HVS-ADJ step-down DC/DC regulator}}
    %DIFAUXCMD
%DIFDELCMD < \label{fig:component_LM2596HVS-ADJ_DC/DC_regulator}
%DIFDELCMD < \end{figure}
%DIFDELCMD < %%%
\DIFdelend %DIF > \begin{figure}[H]
%DIF >     \centering
%DIF >     \includegraphics[width=0.3\textwidth]{0_images/figures/MicroSD card.png}
%DIF >     \caption{SDHC Memory Card}
%DIF >     \label{fig:component_SDHC}
%DIF > \end{figure}

\DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{Power distribution }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{Measurement data will be stored on a microSDHC memory card (SP016GBSTHBU1V10SP) with a capacity of 16~GB. The card has maximum read and write speeds of up to 85~MB/s and 15~MB/s, respectively. The device operates within a supply voltage range of 2.7--3.6~V and an operating temperature range of 0}\degree \DIFadd{C to 70}\degree \DIFadd{C.
}

\DIFadd{The card interfaces with the processing core unit via the SPI (Serial Peripheral Interface), using the MOSI, MISO, SCK, and CS signals for data transfer and control.
}

\subsection{\DIFadd{Power Distribution Board}}

%DIF > \begin{figure}[H]
%DIF >     \centering
%DIF >     \includegraphics[width=0.3\textwidth]{0_images/figures/Stepdown DCDC.png}
%DIF >     \caption{LM2596HVS-ADJ step-down DC/DC regulator}
%DIF >     \label{fig:component_LM2596HVS-ADJ_DC/DC_regulator}
%DIF > \end{figure}
\DIFadd{Power distribution within the experiment }\DIFaddend is handled by \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{multiple }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{a dedicated Power Distribution Board (PDB). The board receives a nominal input voltage of 28.8 V from the BEXUS power system and distributes regulated voltages to the experiment subsystems.
}

\DIFadd{Voltage regulation is implemented using three }\DIFaddend LM2596HVS-ADJ step-down DC/DC \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{regulators}\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{converters}\DIFaddend . These regulators \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{operate at a switching frequency of 150 kHz, }\DIFaddend support input voltages up to 57 V\DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{, }\DIFaddend and provide adjustable output voltages in the range of 1.2\DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{--37 }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{–37 }\DIFaddend V. The \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{SAFT battery supplies a nominal voltage of 28.8 V , which is regulated down to the required systemvoltages of 3.3 V and }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{converters generate three regulated voltage rails required by the experiment: 24 V for the thermal and pressurization systems (compressor and vacuum pumps), }\DIFaddend 8 \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{V}\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{V for the K96 NDIR sensor and the MCU, and 3.3 V for the environmental sensors.
}

\DIFadd{To mitigate switching noise introduced by the DC/DC converters on the shared power lines, an LC filter with a cut-off frequency of approximately 4 kHz is implemented on the power distribution board.
}

\DIFadd{Due to the high startup currents expected from the pressurization system, a pre-charge circuit is implemented on the power distribution board to limit inrush currents during system startup}\DIFaddend .



\subsection{K96 Sensor}

\begin{figure}[H]
    \centering
    \DIFdelbeginFL %DIFDELCMD < \includegraphics[width=0.5\textwidth]{Graphics/Figures/K96 Sensor.png}
%DIFDELCMD <     %%%
\DIFdelendFL \DIFaddbeginFL \includegraphics[width=0.5\textwidth]{0_images/figures/K96 Sensor.png}
    \DIFaddendFL \caption{K96 Sensor. Taken from \cite{wastine2022}}
    \label{fig:component_K96_sensor}
\end{figure}

The K96 sensor is a \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{\mbox{%DIFAUXCMD
\ac{ndir} }\hskip0pt%DIFAUXCMD
}\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{\mbox{%DIFAUXCMD
\gls{ndir} }\hskip0pt%DIFAUXCMD
}\DIFaddend gas sensor capable of measuring methane, carbon dioxide, and water vapor. It operates from a 6.0--8.5 V VBB supply and a 2.4--3.6 V IOVCC/RxD logic supply over a temperature range of $-40\degree$C to +85\degree C, with a maximum peak current of 300 mA. The sensor also provides local temperature, pressure, and humidity measurements via an integrated BME280 sensor.

\subsection{\DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{Pump}%DIFDELCMD < \MBLOCKRIGHTBRACE
%DIFDELCMD < %%%
\DIFdel{The pressurization system will include an electrically driven pump used to increase the pressure inside the measurement chamber. The pump will be powered from the experiment power distribution system and controlled }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{Temperature and Humidity Sensor}}

%DIF > \begin{figure}[H]
%DIF >     \centering
%DIF >     \includegraphics[width=0.3\textwidth]{0_images/figures/Humidity Sensor.png}
%DIF >     \caption{SHT3X-DIS Humidity Sensor}
%DIF >     \label{fig:component_SHT3X-DIS_humidity}
%DIF > \end{figure}

\DIFadd{The ambient temperature and humidity sensor is a SHT45, with an operating range of -40 to 125$^\circ$C and a humidity measurement range of 0--100\% RH. The sensor provides a humidity accuracy of $\pm 1.0$ \% RH and a temperature accuracy of $\pm 0.1\,^\circ$C. Communication with the sensor is performed via an I$^2$C digital interface, allowing temperature and humidity data to be read directly }\DIFaddend by the processing unit. \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{The operating voltage range is 1.08 V to 3.6 V, with a typical average current consumption of approximately 0.4 $\mu$A.
}\DIFaddend 

\DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{At the current design stage, the specific pump model has not yet been selected. Candidate solutions include compact diaphragm- or piston-based pumps capable of operating at low inlet pressures and generating the required pressure differential for chamber pressurization. Final selection will be based on electrical power consumption, achievable pressure ratio, mass, and compatibility with low-temperature operation.
}%DIFDELCMD < 

%DIFDELCMD < %%%
\DIFdel{The pump will be operated in combination with an electronically controlled outlet valve to enable controlled air exchange of the measurement chamber.
Detailed electrical characteristics and control parameters will be defined during the detailed design phase following component selection and testing.
}%DIFDELCMD < 

%DIFDELCMD < %%%
\DIFdelend \subsection{\DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{Ambient }\DIFdelend Pressure Sensor}
\DIFdelbegin %DIFDELCMD <

and precise 24-bit pressure and temperature value. It has \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{I2C }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{I$^2$C }\DIFaddend and SPI interface up to
20MHz and has can measure between 10-1200mbar with an accuracy of \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{$\pm$}\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{±}\DIFaddend 1.5mbar. The
operating temperature range is \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{$-40$ }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{-40 }\DIFaddend to 85\degree C and the supply voltage is between 1.8 and
3.6V

\subsection{\DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{Ambient Temperature Sensor}%DIFDELCMD < \MBLOCKRIGHTBRACE

\DIFadd{The pressurization system is powered through the experiment power distribution board and controlled by the processing unit. The system operates at 24 V and is supplied through the experiment power distribution system.
}\DIFaddend

It has an operating voltage between 3.0 to 5.5V and an active current of 1.5mA}\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{Pressure sensors will be installed between the compression stages and inside the measurement chamber to monitor system performance. These sensors provide feedback to the processing unit, enabling monitoring of the internal pressure and verification of correct system operation}\DIFaddend .

\DIFdelbegin \subsection{\DIFdel{Humidity Sensor}}
%DIFAUXCMD

\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{A passive outlet is used to allow air to exit the measurement chamber. The outlet is implemented as a fixed flow restriction that enables continuous exhaust of air while the pumps and compressor maintain the required chamber pressure. The internal chamber pressure is therefore primarily regulated by the pressurization system, while the passive outlet ensures continuous gas exchange within the chamber.
}\DIFaddend

\DIFdel{C, which in our case could be a problem when we measure at lower temperatures. This needs to be tested or an alternative must be found. The operating voltage is 2.15V to 5.5V with typical current draw of 1.7\micro A}\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{Detailed electrical interfaces, component specifications, and control parameters will be defined during the detailed design phase following component selection and testing}\DIFaddend .

\DIFaddbegin \section{\DIFadd{Thermal Design}}\label{sec: thermal design}
\DIFaddend \begin{figure}[H]
    \centering
    \DIFdelbeginFL %DIFDELCMD < \includegraphics[width=0.3\textwidth]{Graphics/Figures/MicroSD card.png}

    \caption{\DIFaddFL{Averaged pressure and temperature profile obtained by the Sodankylä weather station in Finland over all days of October 2025.}}
    \label{fig:temp_press_profile}
\DIFaddendFL \end{figure}

\DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{To store measurement data, a SanDisk SDSDQAB-008G microSD card will be connected to the processing core unit. It operates at a voltage range of 2.7--3.6 V and supports an operating temperature range of $-25\degree$ C to +85}%DIFDELCMD < \degree %%%
\DIFdel{C.
}%DIFDELCMD < 

%DIFDELCMD < %%%
\section{\DIFdel{Thermal Design}}
%DIFAUXCMD
\addtocounter{section}{-1}%DIFAUXCMD
%DIFDELCMD < 

%DIFDELCMD < %%%
\DIFdelend The temperature conditions in \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{the stratosphere }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{a stratospheric balloon flight }\DIFaddend are quite extreme with temperatures reaching as low as -80\degree C\DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{at the altitudes of about 30 km we could reach}\DIFdelend . At the same time the air pressure can drop as low as 10 mbar, which also implies that convective heat transfer is very weak\DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{and most heat can be dissipated by radiative transfer only. 
This can lead to overheating of the electrical components if the heat from dissipated power accumulates.Therefore, it is essential to know how much power is dissipated and to take additional cooling measures in case that radiative cooling is insufficient. }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{, which might cause a poorly designed system to overheat. An averaged ambient pressure and temperature profile is shown in \fref{fig:temp_press_profile}. The low temperatures might cause substantial convective cooling at lower altitudes of around 8 km, while the low pressure in combination with solar radiation might cause substantial internal and radiative heating. 
In the following, the worst hot and cold cases will be analysed, and expected temperatures of the experiment will be shown by referencing the expected pump operations schedule.}\par 
\DIFaddend 

\DIFaddbegin \subsection{\DIFadd{Thermal analysis background}}
\DIFadd{A detailed description is given in \aref{app:thermal_background}.
}


\subsection{\DIFadd{Cold case}}\label{sec: thermal cold case}
\DIFadd{The cold case is a steady state assumption, given environmental conditions at a specific altitude. The appropriate altitude is determined as the maximum of the air heat convection $Q_\mathrm{conv}(h)$ profile from the averaged temperature and pressure profiles given in \fref{fig:temp_press_profile}. It is assumed that no solar radiation is impacting the system (except \mbox{%DIFAUXCMD
\gls{ir} }\hskip0pt%DIFAUXCMD
radiation from the Earth). Ambient pressure is taken from \fref{fig:temp_press_profile}, while the ambient temperature is set to be $-80$°C. Notably, the tubes containing pressurised, hot air, are assumed to be perfect thermal insulators, which is far from accurate. However, as the heat flux provided by the heated air is very small, this is acceptable.}\par 
\DIFadd{To estimate impact of different operations and system designs, the analysis takes into account whether pumps are turned on or off, whether the experiment has heaters or not and whether the experiment is insulated or not. The insulation is assumed to be a 5 cm thick box of styrofoam, completely encapsulating the chassis. The pumps are assumed to draw 50 W. This corresponds to the maximum power draw possible by two diaphragm pumps and one piston compressor. The active pumps were chosen according to the operations schedule as outlined in \sref{sec: pump system arch}. Assuming an efficiency of 60\% for the diaphragm pumps and 40\% for the piston compressor, the resulting heat load is 26 W.}\par 

\DIFadd{In \tref{tab:thermal results cold case} the results of the cold case analysis are displayed. 
Without the styrofoam insulation, the chassis reaches dangerously low temperatures, even with an active heating system. The heating system then would require over 75 W, which is unfeasible. Even with the styrofoam insulation, the required heating power is around 22 W, which is unsustainable for longer periods of time. However, when the pumps are being used, their heat alone is enough to keep the chassis and chamber within desired temperatures. The cold case analysis therefore concludes a necessity of insulation and active pumps.
}


\DIFaddend \begin{table}[H]
\centering
\DIFdelbeginFL %DIFDELCMD < \caption{%
{%DIFAUXCMD
\DIFdelFL{Component Temperature and Power Ratings}}
%DIFAUXCMD
%DIFDELCMD < \label{tab:component_specs}
%DIFDELCMD < \begin{tabular}{p{4cm}p{1cm}p{1cm}p{1cm}p{1cm}p{1cm}p{1cm}}
%DIFDELCMD < \toprule
%DIFDELCMD < \textbf{Component} &
%DIFDELCMD < \multicolumn{2}{c}{\makecell[c]{Operating T (°C)}} &
%DIFDELCMD < \multicolumn{2}{c}{\makecell[c]{Survivable T (°C)}} &
%DIFDELCMD < \multicolumn{2}{c}{\makecell[c]{Power Dissipated (W)}} \\
%DIFDELCMD < \cmidrule(lr){2-3} \cmidrule(lr){4-5} \cmidrule(lr){6-7}
%DIFDELCMD < & Min & Max & Min & Max & Mean & Max \\

%DIFDELCMD < \end{tabular}
%DIFDELCMD < %%%
\DIFdelendFL \DIFaddbeginFL \caption{\DIFaddFL{Thermal Performance Analysis: Cold Case Scenarios at 8.8\,km Altitude}}
\label{tab:thermal results cold case}
\begin{tabular}{@{}lll S[table-format=-2.2] S[table-format=-2.2] S[table-format=-2.2]@{}}
\toprule
\textbf{Insulation} & \textbf{Pumps} & \textbf{Thermal System} & {$T_{Chassis}$ [\si{\celsius}]} & {$T_{Chamber}$ [\si{\celsius}]} & {Power [\si{W}]} \\ \midrule
None & Off & None & -79.27 & -79.27 & {---} \\
None & Off & Active & -29.02 & 21.00 & 75.23 \\ 
Styrofoam & Off & Passive & -78.70 & -78.70 & {---} \\
Styrofoam & Off & Active & 6.52 & 21.00 & 21.79 \\ \midrule
None & Active & None & -61.80 & -61.80 & {---} \\
None & Active & Active & -20.40 & 21.00 & 62.28 \\ 
Styrofoam & Active & Passive & -72.41 & -72.41 & {---} \\
Styrofoam & Active & Active & 21.14 & 21.00 & -0.21 \\ \bottomrule
\addlinespace
\multicolumn{6}{l}{\small \textit{Note: Negative power values indicate active cooling required to maintain target temperature.}}
\end{tabular}
\DIFaddendFL \end{table}

\DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{The experiment can be thermally divided into three parts. The coldest part are the ambient sensors on the outside of the experiment box. They are }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \subsection{\DIFadd{Hot case}}\label{sec: thermal hot case}
\DIFadd{Similar to the cold case, the hot case will follow a lumped system approach. However, the appropriate altitude here is chosen to minimize the impact of heat convection via air and maximizing solar heating. This means that the hot case will assume environmental conditions like those found at 30 km altitude, namely $T=-66$°C, $p=0.01$ bar and cloud-free skies. As in the cold case analysis, the effects of pumps, active heating, and styrofoam insulation are tested for individually. The results are displayed in \tref{tab:thermal results hot case}. Without styrofoam insulation, and no thermal system/heating or active pumps, the chassis temperature will reach around $-3$°C. 
Adding the insulation leads to a counterintuitive temperature reduction to $-47$°C. This is due to the styrofoam reflecting more sunlight than the aluminium, while simultaneously loosing heat more efficiently via \mbox{%DIFAUXCMD
\gls{ir} }\hskip0pt%DIFAUXCMD
radiation. 
As the heat loads involved are comparatively small, an active heating system has to provide around 14 W to raise the chamber temperature to 21°C. However, if pumps are active at 30 km altitude, the chassis will quickly reach temperatures higher than 21°C, requiring cooling of around 9 W instead.}\par 

\DIFadd{In conclusion, the hot case analysis shows that active pumps at high altitudes will necessitate a cooling system. In order to avoid complexity, it is therefore favourable to avoid using the pumps at high altitudes. 
This agrees well with the planned operations for the pumps, as given in \sref{sec: pump system arch}.
}

 


\begin{table}[H]
\centering
\caption{\DIFaddFL{Thermal Performance Analysis: Hot Case Scenarios at 30\,km Altitude}}
\label{tab:thermal results hot case}
\begin{tabular}{@{}lll S[table-format=-2.2] S[table-format=-2.2] S[table-format=-2.2]@{}}
\toprule
\textbf{Insulation} & \textbf{Pumps} & \textbf{Thermal System} & {$T_{Chassis}$ [\si{\celsius}]} & {$T_{Chamber}$ [\si{\celsius}]} & {Power [\si{W}]} \\ \midrule
None & Off & None & -3.16 & -3.16 & {---} \\
None & Off & Active & 16.39 & 21.00 & 6.93 \\ 
Styrofoam & Off & Passive & -47.32 & -47.32 & {---} \\
Styrofoam & Off & Active & 11.89 & 21.00 & 13.71 \\ \midrule
None & Active & None & 65.10 & 65.10 & {---} \\
None & Active & Active & 30.25 & 21.00 & -13.92 \\ 
Styrofoam & Active & Passive & -29.47 & -29.47 & {---} \\
Styrofoam & Active & Active & 26.75 & 21.00 & -8.65 \\ \bottomrule
\addlinespace

\end{tabular}
\end{table}


\subsection{\DIFadd{Continuous operation}}
\DIFadd{As before, the continous operation usses a lumped system approach. However, now the total energy of the system is not assumed to be constant, but transient. This means that the system temperature is changing with the changing environmental and internal heat loads. Therefore, thermal inertia needs to be taken into account via
}\begin{align}
    \DIFadd{mc_{p}\dot{T} =}& \DIFadd{Q_\mathrm{ele}+Q_\mathrm{pump}(h)+Q_\mathrm{transfer} + Q_\mathrm{rad\,in}(h) + Q_\mathrm{insulation} - Q_\mathrm{conv}(h) - Q_\mathrm{rad\,out} \label{eq: heat diff chassis}
}\end{align}
\DIFadd{for the chassis. As before, the power required to keep the pressure chamber at 21°C can be calculated via \eqref{eq:heat_balance_chamber}, since $\dot{T}$ is supposed to be equal to 0 for the chamber. 
In this scenario the pumps operate by the operating plan specified in \sref{sec: pump system arch}. Additionally, pumps and thermal systems are activated 30 minutes before launch to simulate pre-heating }\DIFaddend and \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{should be completely exposed and therefore are required to withstand temperaturesas low as -80}%DIFDELCMD < \degree %%%
\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{pre-pressurisation of the chamber. Otherwise the pumps are assumed to work on lower power draw settings at altitudes under 14 km, adding an estimated heat load of 18 W. Between 14 km and 22 km, the pumps operate at full power, adding a heat load of 26 W. Beyond 22 km the pumps are switched off. The total flight time is estimated to be 6 h long. 
The float phase is assumed to be at constant 26 km altitude. The heating system is assumed to be capable of a maximum heating output of 10 W.
After the pumps are deactivated at 22 km, the heating system aims to stabilise the chamber temperature at $-5$°}\DIFaddend C. \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{This poses a challenge since most commercially available components are made for ground level conditions and manufacturers usually draw the line at -40}%DIFDELCMD < \degree %%%
\DIFdel{Cfor the temperature conditions. 
If we should get access to a thermal chamber , we can test the reliability of those sensors ourselves at lower temperatures. Since we haven't found sensors with operating ranges going down as low as -80}%DIFDELCMD < \degree %%%
\DIFdel{C we might have to resort to this attempt. 
Those sensors are not central to our experiment. The second part is the electronics compartment, which will likely be not actively heated. The bigger issue for this part might be overheating instead and a careful thermal analysis during the design process will be required to decide on mitigation steps if the temperature ranges of the components are exceeded at the upper or possibly still lower bounds. Later, in the integration process, further tests will follow to verify the functionality of that part at high altitudes. }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{This is done as a proxy to prevent the electronics in the chassis from freezing.}\par 
\DIFaddend 

The \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{last part is }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{result for an experiment inside a styrofoam insulation box and subject to solar daylight is shown in \fref{fig:continous thermal sim main}. 
%DIF > As expected, heating is especially necessary between ca 8 km and 14 km. 
The chassis temperature never drops much below $-10$°C, while the thermal power requirements do not reach the set maximum of 10 W.  
By integrating the power requirement curve one obtains a total required heating power of $\approx 29.45$ Wh. This result refers to heating energy and not to }\DIFaddend the \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{measurement chamber of which the temperature needs to be more carefully regulated to ensure that the K96 \mbox{%DIFAUXCMD
\ac{ndir} }\hskip0pt%DIFAUXCMD
sensor}\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{energy necessary to power heating components}\DIFaddend , which is \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{central to our experiment, functions properly.Temperature fluctuations can also lead to output drift and therefore we need }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{expected to be greater.}\par 
\DIFadd{If no insulation were used, the total required heating power would jump to 37.45 Wh. If insulation were used, but no daylight were available, the heating power required would be 36.42 Wh. The temperature and heating curves for both cases are provided in \fref{fig:continous thermal sim side}. If no insulation is used, the heating system is unable }\DIFaddend to keep the \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{temperature in a narrow range . At the same time, pumps, valves or inlet and outlet tube openings could freeze over if the humidity level is high.
Since we might fly through clouds with very high humidity levels it is essential that the relative humidity doesn't get too high. By increasing the temperature }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{chamber temperature within the required temperature range of 15°C to 40°C during ascend. It also fails at keeping the chassis temperature within a safe range }\DIFaddend of \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{-15 to 65°C during descend. On the other hand, if insulation is used, but the flight is during night time or particularly cloudy conditions, the effects are rather minimal. The heater would max out its power output and }\DIFaddend the \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{air, condensation can be mitigated and freezing of pumps, valves and tubes should be prevented as well }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{chassis temperature would drop to around $-15$°C during descend, which is just the lower limit of the safe range and still 10°C away from the strict lower limit of -25°C.
}

\begin{figure}[htbp]
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=0.8\textwidth]{0_images/figures/thermal_analysis_plot_WITHins_WITHlight.png}
    \caption{\DIFaddFL{Expected temperature of the chassis and chamber and the heating power necessary to sustain 21°C in the chamber. The gray lines indicate the time intervals when the balloon is ascending, floating, and descending. The colors indicate different power settings of the pressurisation system: green - no pumps, yellow - low power, red - high power.}}
    \label{fig:continous thermal sim main}
\end{figure}

\subsection{\DIFadd{Conclusion}}\label{sec: thermal conclusion}
\DIFadd{The cold and hot case analysis shows that the experiment if enclosed in a styrofoam insulation is able to stay within the required temperature range depending on the proper use of pumps. In the ascend phase, the pumps are generating enough heat to keep the chamber temperature at 21°C, even without additional heating elements. In fact the transientt analysis shows that temperatures of chassis and chamber might reach close to 40°C. During float phase, the chamber temperature is allowed to drop to $-5$°C. This is done in order to keep the electrical components within the chassis at around $-10$°C. The required total heating power for daytime and nighttime would be $\approx 29.45$ Wh and $\approx 36.42$ Wh, respectively. Depending on the final design of the electrical components, the temperature limit and thus required power might be lowered further.
}

\subsection{\DIFadd{Passive thermal control system}}
\DIFadd{The above analysis shows that white styrofoam is a good candidate for insulation. The chassis is planned to be completely sourrounded by 5 cm thick styrofoam insulation. Further analysis on other insulation materials and implementations, as well as on other passive control systems, will commence with the start of thesis project 6 and 7 (see \tref{tab:thesis}). Additionally, the intake air will be preheated before being fed to the diaphragm pumps. This can be done via a thermally conductive, solenoid shaped tubing housed inside the chassis. If the tubing has higher heat capacity than the incoming air, it can act as a heat sink, heating the incoming air to an acceptable temperature. The exact shape and length of the tube will be determined via tests}\DIFaddend .
\DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{Further tests will be conducted in later stages of experiment integration}\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin 

\subsection{\DIFadd{Active thermal control system}}
\DIFadd{The above analysis shows that during ascend and before launch, the pumps alone will provide enough heating power to heat the chamber to around 40°C. This is on the edge of the acceptable temperature range as outlined in \tref{tab:design_req}, P.7. Further analysis of overheating mitigation methods, including pump duty cycles and other active thermal control methods will commence with the start of thesis project 6 (see \tref{tab:thesis}).}\par 

\DIFadd{During float and descend, the above analysis shows that a 10 W heater is sufficient to keep the electronics inside the chassis within acceptable limits, see \tref{tab:design_req}, D.1.3. There are multiple common choices for thermal control components at low wattages and high precision. These will be discussed below.
}\paragraph{\DIFadd{\mbox{%DIFAUXCMD
\gls{ptc} }\hskip0pt%DIFAUXCMD
ceramics}} \DIFadd{are commonly used in portable space heaters. They are used via resistive heating. However, due to their \mbox{%DIFAUXCMD
\gls{ptc}}\hskip0pt%DIFAUXCMD
, a rising internal temperature causes their resistivity to increase as well. This behaviour can be configured, such that a desired maximum temperature can be set at which the ceramic resistivity becomes too high for the temperature to rise further. They are therefore self regulating, adding additional security and requiring less controlling. They are also quite cheap. However, because they are made of ceramic, they can not easily fit inside a tight experiment}\DIFaddend . 

\DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{Insulation will play another essential role in the thermal design. The whole experiment should be as much thermally separated from the rest of }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \paragraph{\DIFadd{Peltier devices}} \DIFadd{are heat pumps, that transfer heat from one side of the device to the other when an electrical current is applied. Upon reversal of the current polarity, the heat transfer flow is also reversed. These devices can therefore be used to cool and heat. This makes them great candidates for an active thermal control system that has to cool and heat components depending on mission phase. The downside is that they require heat sinks that the excess heat (or lack thereof) can be dumped to. They also require more sophisticated controlling software and circuits to be able to switch between cooling and heating.
}

\paragraph{\DIFadd{Polyimide film heating elements}} \DIFadd{consist of a thin conductive heating element sourrounded by polyimide film. They are very flexible, sturdy and commonly used in space environment heating solutions. Although they are not self regulating, they can be easily controlled using \mbox{%DIFAUXCMD
\gls{pid} }\hskip0pt%DIFAUXCMD
algorithms. They are unable to provide cooling, but offer a great surface area to enable uniform heating. Due to their flexibility, they are also easy to integrate into a system and do not require complex software or circuits.}\par  

\DIFadd{Because of the low heating power required, the small operating temperature expected, the ease of integration, its big surface area, and its low cost a polyimide film heating element is chosen. The heating element is included in \tref{tab:power_consumption}. It can be attached via \mbox{%DIFAUXCMD
\gls{psa} }\hskip0pt%DIFAUXCMD
and wrapped around the pressure chamber. Since the chamber is made out of aluminium, and aluminium is good thermal conductor, one heating pad should suffice. This will be confirmed by tests. Should tests show that there is indeed no need for heating the pressurisation chamber, the heater may be moved directly to }\DIFaddend the \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{vehicle as possible. On one hand, this avoids furthercooling of the experimentand on }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{temperature sensitive electrical components like the \mbox{%DIFAUXCMD
\gls{pcb}}\hskip0pt%DIFAUXCMD
.}\par 

\DIFadd{The heating pad will be controlled using a \mbox{%DIFAUXCMD
\gls{pid} }\hskip0pt%DIFAUXCMD
algorithm, as common for heating appliances. The algorithm will run on the \mbox{%DIFAUXCMD
\gls{mcu}}\hskip0pt%DIFAUXCMD
. The \mbox{%DIFAUXCMD
\gls{mcu} }\hskip0pt%DIFAUXCMD
then controls the heating power of the heating pad by adjusting its duty cycle by powering a \mbox{%DIFAUXCMD
\gls{mosfet}}\hskip0pt%DIFAUXCMD
. The exact \mbox{%DIFAUXCMD
\gls{pwm} }\hskip0pt%DIFAUXCMD
setting is to be determined by the final \mbox{%DIFAUXCMD
\gls{mcu} }\hskip0pt%DIFAUXCMD
design. To prevent overheating of }\DIFaddend the \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{other it avoids impacting other experiment or the vehicle. The measurement chambershould be well insulated as well to not (further) heat up the electronics compartment and to avoid condensation of water on the walls of the chamber}\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{chamber, a \mbox{%DIFAUXCMD
\gls{tco} }\hskip0pt%DIFAUXCMD
unit is used, wired in series with the heating pad. It can be directly attached via \mbox{%DIFAUXCMD
\gls{psa} }\hskip0pt%DIFAUXCMD
to the outside of the pressure chamber, monitoring its temperature and breaking the circuit when its temperature reaches above 50°C}\DIFaddend .


%DIF >  This can lead to overheating of the electrical components if the heat from dissipated power accumulates. Therefore, it is essential to know how much power is dissipated and to take additional cooling measures in case that radiative cooling is insufficient.
\DIFaddbegin

%DIF >  \multicolumn{2}{c}{\makecell[c]{Power Dissipated (W)}} \\
%DIF >  \cmidrule(lr){2-3} \cmidrule(lr){4-5} \cmidrule(lr){6-7}

%DIF >  \midrule
%DIF >  Pump &  &  &  &  &  &    \\

%DIF >  \end{table}

%DIF >  The experiment can be thermally divided into three parts. The coldest part are the ambient sensors on the outside of the experiment box. They are and should be completely exposed and therefore are required to withstand temperatures as low as -80\degree C. This poses a challenge since most commercially available components are made for ground level conditions and manufacturers usually draw the line at -40\degree C for the temperature conditions. If we should get access to a thermal chamber, we can test the reliability of those sensors ourselves at lower temperatures. Since we haven't found sensors with operating ranges going down as low as -80\degree C we might have to resort to this attempt. Those sensors are not central to our experiment. The second part is the electronics compartment, which will likely be not actively heated. The bigger issue for this part might be overheating instead and a careful thermal analysis during the design process will be required to decide on mitigation steps if the temperature ranges of the components are exceeded at the upper or possibly still lower bounds. Later, in the integration process, further tests will follow to verify the functionality of that part at high altitudes.

%DIF >  The last part is the measurement chamber of which the temperature needs to be more carefully regulated to ensure that the K96 \gls{ndir} sensor, which is central to our experiment, functions properly. Temperature fluctuations can also lead to output drift and therefore we need to keep the temperature in a narrow range. At the same time, pumps, valves or inlet and outlet tube openings could freeze over if the humidity level is high. Since we might fly through clouds with very high humidity levels it is essential that the relative humidity doesn't get too high. By increasing the temperature of the air, condensation can be mitigated and freezing of pumps, valves and tubes should be prevented as well. Further tests will be conducted in later stages of experiment integration.

%DIF >  Insulation will play another essential role in the thermal design. The whole experiment should be as much thermally separated from the rest of the vehicle as possible. On one hand, this avoids further cooling of the experiment and on the other it avoids impacting other experiment or the vehicle. The measurement chamber should be well insulated as well to not (further) heat up the electronics compartment and to avoid condensation of water on the walls of the chamber.

%DIF > %%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
\DIFaddend \section{Power \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{System}%DIFDELCMD < \MBLOCKRIGHTBRACE
%DIFDELCMD < %%%
\DIFdel{The \mbox{%DIFAUXCMD
\ac{mirage} }\hskip0pt%DIFAUXCMD
}\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{design}}

\DIFadd{The \mbox{%DIFAUXCMD
\gls{mirage} }\hskip0pt%DIFAUXCMD
}\DIFaddend team expects to rely exclusively on the 28.8V SAFT MP176065 which is provided by the gondola.

The experiment \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{estimates 0.35A from Bexus power unit for all the component excluding the }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{will have an estimated average current of 0.80A and use 138.67Wh in total, see table 4.9, including the thermal system and the pressurization system. This is higher than previous estimations, mainly due to the }\DIFaddend thermal system, \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{which is yet to be decided}\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{compressor and vacuum pumps, but this calculation is based on full capacity so it's expected to be lower than this}\DIFaddend . The peak current draw \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{from our power distribution board is estimated to be 1.15 A and is calculated with max current draw of each components. The Arduino mega has a max current of 200 mA which is much higher tham what is expected during the experiment. The }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{is high at 2.26A from the BEXUS side for ~30 minutes, see table 4.8, while the compressor and vacuum pumps are working at full capacity, which is close to the requirement D.11.1. 
Since the MCU PCB will be designed by thesis students it is difficult to estimate precisely the current draw for this component, however it was estimated to be 300mA. The }\DIFaddend K96 \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{sensor }\DIFdelend has a max current of \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{300 mA }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{300mA }\DIFaddend but the expected average is \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{much }\DIFdelend lower depending on the time per measurement\DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{, the time for flushing and pressurization . The pump is expected to be supplied with a maximum of 500 mA and the average current will depend on the time per measurement }\DIFdelend . 
\DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{The SDHC card has an average current of 20 mA and a maximum of 150 mA}\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin 

\DIFadd{Before launch, the pressurization system, e.g. vacuum pumps and compressor currents will be limited using some sort of pre-charge circuit and will thus not have a major impact on the energy consumption as a whole. During the flight phase, the pressurization system will be activated in lesser capacity for $\approx 45$ minutes and in full capacity $\approx 30$ minutes in order to conserve energy for the rest of the experiment. After the measurement phase the pressurization system will be limited or completely shut off.
}

\DIFadd{The thermal system will not be activated while the pressurization system is active in order to not exceed current draw limitations and thus will only be operational as detailed in thermal design section}\DIFaddend .

\DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{Table 4.9 presents key components, amount of components, duration of use, voltage levels, currents and power. The table is representing from the experimenters perspective while the average and total BEXUS power is from the BEXUS power unit perspective. This calculations used the assumption of $85\%$ efficiency to calculate the power consumtion on the BEXUS power unit. 
%DIF > %%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
}\DIFaddend \section{Software \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{Design}\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{design}\DIFaddend }

The on-board software is responsible for collecting data from the \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{K96 sensor core }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{sensors }\DIFaddend and store it on the on-board SD card. It is also responsible for handling communication from and to ground, as well as the control of the internal \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{pumping }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{pressure }\DIFaddend and thermal systems \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{and preventing condensation}\DIFaddend .

\DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{For the pressurisation system the software needs to be able to read out the pressure sensor in the measurement chamber and adjust the power of the pumps accordingly to maintain a constant pressure of 3 bar. The mechanical side of the system is explained above. Since the vacuum pumps prepressurise the air that is then compressed to 3 bar, the power of the vacuum pumps and the compressor need to be handled separately. Depending on whether the compressor needs to have a minimum flowrate to work, it might be possible that a pulsed operation is necessary for the compressor, which would put strain on the electrical system. The software needs to be able to adjust these different components accordingly based on the internal pressure sensor. }\\

\DIFadd{For the thermal control system the software needs to take sensor inputs from different places. It should be split into two different programs, where one is responsible for stabilizing the temperature in the measurement chamber at around room temperature. This also includes controlling potential active heating elements at the gas inlet of the outer enclosure and before the air at 3 bar enters the measurement chamber based on at least on the thermal sensor of the K96 but possibly also several thermal sensor in between. The other thermal control loop should monitor components that are at risk of overheating and components that could underheat. There might be also active heating elements at critical components like the MCU, which could be switched on if the temperatures drop dangerously low inside the electrical compartment. This control loop need to take the different temperature outputs of sensors that are close to the pumps and electrical components and if necessary take counter measures via controlling the heating elements or reducing the power of the pumps.}\\
\DIFadd{For humidity the role of the software is to detect to high humidity levels (>80\% r.H.). If the ambient sensors detect high levels of humidity outside the software should close the shutter and stop the pumps as a first step to prevent more air with high humidity levels from entering the measurement chamber without losing pressure. When the humidity levels are low enough, the temperature of the air taken into the measurement chamber should be increased slightly by increasing the temperature of the active heating elements until the internal humidity is lower as well.}\\


\DIFaddend The settings of those systems depend on the readings of the internal and external pressure and temperature sensors. Additionally, correct flight phases need to be engaged. This is done based on longer term changes in the recorded ambient pressure or via a command from the ground, with the command taking priority. A \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{information flow and process flow block }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{control flow and state }\DIFaddend diagram are provided in Figure \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{\ref{fig:info_flow} and Figure \ref{fig:process_flow}}\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{\ref{fig:cont_flow} and Figure \ref{fig:state_diagram}}\DIFaddend .

\DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{The experiment will run on 2 main modes: Manual and Autonomous. Each of the main modes has a submode for standby and active measurements. When the experiment reaches the point where no more data needs to be collected, the system will move to standby mode unless a command has been given to do otherwise. The modes will also allow for autonomous work even through radio blackouts and loss of communication. If below 22\,km and a loss of connection occurs, the system will automatically switch to autonomous measurements mode; if however the experiment is above 22\,km and a loss of connection occurs then the system will switch to Autonomous standby mode that will ensure the experiment remains safe without exhausting any of the hardware.
}

\DIFaddend To guarantee operations \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{multiple \mbox{%DIFAUXCMD
\ac{mcu} }\hskip0pt%DIFAUXCMD
will be included in the experiment with one being active while the other serving as a watchdog and can overtake operations if needed}\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{watchdog systems will be used to reset the operations if it encountered a fatal error.
The software system will work with different threads working asynchronously.
Data transfer between the threads will happen through producer-consumer queues that are thread-safe.
There will be a thread for reading sensor data, a thread of controlling hardware, a thread sending downlink packages and receiving packages from uplink, a thread to write data on the SD card and finally a thread to control everything with the aid of a watchdog}\DIFaddend .

All transmissions are saved to the on-board storage with timestamps. Similarly, all saved data is saved with timestamps, too. A table detailing the expected data sizes of data to be stored is given in Table \ref{tab:data_rates}. \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{This estimates around 3 kbps of storeddata for a total duration of around }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{In total 39856 bits per second (40 kbit) will be stored. This means that for the duration of }\DIFaddend 6 hours \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{, giving a total storage requirement of 64.8 Mbit. Ideally the whole data is sent down in regular intervals once it is recorded. }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{a storage of 108 MB (megabyte) is required to store all the data received from the sensors. The SD cards onboard are more than enough for this task. Some of this data will be downlinked every seconds for redundancy and tracking of the functionality of the experiment. }\DIFaddend Based on the sampling rates in Table \ref{tab:data_rates},\DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{an interval of every 30 seconds seems reasonable. Thus, every 30 seconds, 91.5 kbit need to be transmit, ideally within 10 seconds to provide room for sending checksums and possibly duplicates. Therefore, we estimate a normal data rate of 9.15 kbps}\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{the Elink thread can pull 100 datapoints from the telemetry and data queue to downlink each second, resulting in an estimated downlink rate of 32.8 kbps, below the experiment requirements (P.18) of 100 kbps}\DIFaddend . Assuming a maximum connection dropout duration of 10 minutes, this would likewise imply a build-up of data of \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{1.8 }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{19.68 }\DIFaddend Mbit. Transferring it over the next 5 minutes gives a data rate of \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{60 kbps }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{65.6 kbps which is feasible}\DIFaddend . With compression it is probable that only half the rates stated need to be transmitted, but a higher data rate could be used to send duplicates or checksums for error mitigation.

The \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{frequencies are chosen based on the datasheets of the sensors. The SHT45 sensor has multiple modes, fast mode plus (FM+) was chosen for this mission. The }\DIFaddend K96 sensor \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{relies on the speed of the NDIR sensor within with typically is below 10 Hz, whereas in the MS5607 sensor OSR 4096 mode was chosen. The \mbox{%DIFAUXCMD
\gls{rtc} }\hskip0pt%DIFAUXCMD
frequency was calculated to be equal to all the frequencies added. This is to consider the scenario where none of the sensors data readings collide and all the readings from the 3 sensors are collected at different timestamps, which will require one timestamp for each.
}

\DIFadd{The K96 sensor }\DIFaddend core requires a UART interface and relies on the Modbus protocol\DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{, while most other sensors use I2C}\DIFaddend . The communication protocol will be UDP.

\begin{table}[H]
\centering
\caption{\DIFdelbeginFL \DIFdelFL{Sampling }\DIFdelendFL \DIFaddbeginFL \DIFaddFL{Max sampling }\DIFaddendFL rates, sampling sizes, and data rates of data products to be stored}
\label{tab:data_rates}
\DIFdelbeginFL %DIFDELCMD < \begin{tabular}{p{7cm}p{2cm}p{2cm}p{2cm}}
%DIFDELCMD < \toprule
%DIFDELCMD < \textbf{Component} & \textbf{Sampling rate [Hz]} & \textbf{Sampling size [bit]} & \textbf{Data rate [bps]} \\
%DIFDELCMD < \midrule
%DIFDELCMD < K96 concentration readings & 0.05 & 192 & 9.6 \\
%DIFDELCMD < K96 housekeeping data & 1 & 96 & 96 \\
%DIFDELCMD < Internal pressure sensor & 10 & 32 & 320 \\
%DIFDELCMD < Internal temperature sensor & 10 & 32 & 320 \\
%DIFDELCMD < Internal humidity sensor & 10 & 32 & 320 \\

%DIFDELCMD < Ambient temperature sensor & 10 & 32 & 320 \\
%DIFDELCMD < Ambient humidity sensor & 10 & 32 & 320 \\
%DIFDELCMD < General housekeeping data (current for e.g. pumps, data from thermal system, ...) & 1 & 384 & 384 \\
%DIFDELCMD < \midrule
%DIFDELCMD < Total & & & 3049.6 \\
%DIFDELCMD < \bottomrule

\section{Ground \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{Support Equipment}\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{support equipment}\DIFaddend }
\DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{The data will be primarily saved onboard; however for redundancy and system check, the team will use the E-link. ESRANGE is equiped with a LIDAR device, which could be used in order to measure the vertical methane concentration for later validation}\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin 

\DIFadd{A \mbox{%DIFAUXCMD
\gls{gui} }\hskip0pt%DIFAUXCMD
will be developed for easy control of \mbox{%DIFAUXCMD
\gls{mirage} }\hskip0pt%DIFAUXCMD
during the mission. The \mbox{%DIFAUXCMD
\gls{gui} }\hskip0pt%DIFAUXCMD
will allow ground operators to send commands through uplink and visualize the downlink data. Additionally the team will require basic electronic equipment, chairs and a table to operate from and troubleshoot any issues before flight}\DIFaddend .

Simulate ascent in duration and rate of pressure decline. Keep at 1 mbar for at least 15 minutes. Perform cyclic pressurisation of measurement chamber during the whole time with measurement time corresponding to pressure level. \\
%DIFDELCMD < Test campaign duration & 2 days (setup and execution) \\
%DIFDELCMD < Test campaign date & May \\
%DIFDELCMD < Test completed & No \\
%DIFDELCMD < Requirements verified & \\

\subsection{Flight Requirements}
\DIFdelbegin %DIFDELCMD < 

%DIFDELCMD < \begin{table}[h]
%DIFDELCMD < %%%
\DIFdelendFL \DIFaddbeginFL \begin{table}[htbp]
\DIFaddendFL \centering
\caption{Flight Requirements applicable to BEXUS}
\label{tab:flight_req_bexus}
\DIFdelbeginFL %DIFDELCMD < \begin{tabular}{p{5cm} p{10cm}}
%DIFDELCMD < \toprule
%DIFDELCMD < \textbf{Optimal altitude} & $>$25km\\
%DIFDELCMD < \textbf{Preferred path} & ascent \\
%DIFDELCMD < \textbf{Minimum float time} & 2 hours\\
%DIFDELCMD < \textbf{Ground track length} & $>$200km\\
%DIFDELCMD < \textbf{Light/dark condition} & no preference but should be uniform suring flight\\
%DIFDELCMD < \bottomrule
%DIFDELCMD < \end{tabular}
%DIFDELCMD < %%%
\DIFdelendFL \DIFaddbeginFL \begin{tabular}{p{5cm} p{10cm}}
\toprule
\textbf{Optimal altitude} & 22km \\
\textbf{Preferred path} & ascent \\
\textbf{Minimum float time} & not important\\
\textbf{Ground track length} & not important\\
\textbf{Light/dark condition} & no preference but should be uniform during ascent if possible\\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\DIFaddendFL \end{table}
\clearpage

%DIF > %%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
\subsection{Accommodation Requirements}
\DIFaddbegin 

\DIFaddend The experiment needs access to fresh air and the gas in- and outlets should not be covered by the canvas\DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{and they }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{. They }\DIFaddend should be directed away from the vehicle and other experiments to minimize interference \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{. 
}\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{and contamination of the intaken air. Therefore the experiment should be accommodated in a corner of the gondola.
}\DIFaddend

\section{Post-Flight Activities}

\DIFdelbegin \chapter{\DIFdel{Data Analysis And Results}}

\DIFaddbegin \chapter{\DIFadd{DATA ANALYSIS AND RESULTS}}
\label{sec:data_analysis_results}

\DIFaddend \section{Data Analysis Plan}

The focus of attention for \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{\mbox{%DIFAUXCMD
\ac{mirage} }\hskip0pt%DIFAUXCMD
}\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{\mbox{%DIFAUXCMD
\gls{mirage} }\hskip0pt%DIFAUXCMD
}\DIFaddend is to demonstrate the applicability of the \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{\mbox{%DIFAUXCMD
\ac{ndir} }\hskip0pt%DIFAUXCMD
}\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{\mbox{%DIFAUXCMD
\gls{ndir} }\hskip0pt%DIFAUXCMD
}\DIFaddend sensor for low-cost and low-complexity methane measurements in the stratosphere.
Since the biggest trade-off is resolution in this endeavour, \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{we will assess }\DIFdelend the uncertainty of \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{our measurement }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{the measurement will be assessed }\DIFaddend with great care.
A lot of this will be done before the flight by carefully calibrating the sensor in the temperature and pressure range \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{we plan on setting in }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{of }\DIFaddend the measurement chamber for different methane concentrations and humidity levels.
If possible, \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{we will also train }\DIFdelend a machine learning algorithm \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{in this process }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{will be trained }\DIFaddend to reduce the impact of noise on \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{our measurements.
}%DIFDELCMD < 

%DIFDELCMD < %%%
\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{the measurements, similar to \mbox{%DIFAUXCMD
\cite{gia_accurate_2025}}\hskip0pt%DIFAUXCMD
. }\DIFaddend This is an important prerequisite for the quality of the data \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{we can collect }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{collected }\DIFaddend during the flight.

To further demonstrate the reliability of \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{our }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{the }\DIFaddend measurement data, the focus of post-flight data analysis is on comparison of the vertical methane profile from ascent and descent with other sources of methane data.
For this \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{we want }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{it is intended }\DIFaddend to use:

\begin{enumerate}
    \item Radiative transfer models (like RTTOV)
    \item Chemistry and Transport models (like MOCAGE)
    \item Satellite data
    \item In-situ measurement data from other groups
\DIFdelbegin %DIFDELCMD < \item %%%
\item%DIFAUXCMD
\DIFdel{In-situ measurement with LIDAR at ESRANGE
}\DIFdelend \end{enumerate}

\DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{\mbox{%DIFAUXCMD
\ac{rtm} }\hskip0pt%DIFAUXCMD
}\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{\mbox{%DIFAUXCMD
\gls{rtm} }\hskip0pt%DIFAUXCMD
}\DIFaddend like RTTOV can provide reference concentration profiles of atmospheric gases like methane and have been compared to \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{\mbox{%DIFAUXCMD
\ac{tdlas} }\hskip0pt%DIFAUXCMD
}\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{\mbox{%DIFAUXCMD
\gls{tdlas} }\hskip0pt%DIFAUXCMD
}\DIFaddend measurements before.
\DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{\mbox{%DIFAUXCMD
\ac{ctm} }\hskip0pt%DIFAUXCMD
}\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{\mbox{%DIFAUXCMD
\gls{ctm} }\hskip0pt%DIFAUXCMD
}\DIFaddend like MOCAGE assimilate satellite data into their calculations and give an additional gauge of what \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{we should expect for our data }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{should be expected for the data collected by MIRAGE}\DIFaddend , although they have shown worse correspondence with in-situ measurements \cite{joly2020}.
The use of satellite data in addition to their indirect use via \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{\mbox{%DIFAUXCMD
\ac{ctm} }\hskip0pt%DIFAUXCMD
}\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{\mbox{%DIFAUXCMD
\gls{ctm} }\hskip0pt%DIFAUXCMD
}\DIFaddend needs to be assessed.
This and the familiarisation with \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{\mbox{%DIFAUXCMD
\ac{rtm} }\hskip0pt%DIFAUXCMD
and \mbox{%DIFAUXCMD
\ac{ctm} }\hskip0pt%DIFAUXCMD
}\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{\mbox{%DIFAUXCMD
\gls{rtm} }\hskip0pt%DIFAUXCMD
and \mbox{%DIFAUXCMD
\gls{ctm} }\hskip0pt%DIFAUXCMD
}\DIFaddend will be part of a bachelor thesis project within \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{\mbox{%DIFAUXCMD
\ac{mirage}}\hskip0pt%DIFAUXCMD
}\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{\mbox{%DIFAUXCMD
\gls{mirage}}\hskip0pt%DIFAUXCMD
}\DIFaddend , that is planned for several team members.

In-situ measurements were conducted with \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{\mbox{%DIFAUXCMD
\ac{tdlas} }\hskip0pt%DIFAUXCMD
}\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{\mbox{%DIFAUXCMD
\gls{tdlas} }\hskip0pt%DIFAUXCMD
}\DIFaddend from ESRANGE in 2016 and provide a direct comparison with more expensive but also more accurate laser-based methods \cite{Khair2017}.
The comparison is of course limited because of the different times at which measurements were done.
\DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{We still expect }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{It is still expected }\DIFaddend to get some interesting insights by including these sources into \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{our }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{the }\DIFaddend data analysis.
Another in-situ measurement campaign conducted over France with AirCore in 2018 compared their data to \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{\mbox{%DIFAUXCMD
\ac{ftir} }\hskip0pt%DIFAUXCMD
}\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{\mbox{%DIFAUXCMD
\gls{ftir} }\hskip0pt%DIFAUXCMD
}\DIFaddend measurements \cite{RINGO}.
\DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{This is an interesting study for us as well, especially if ESRANGE can support us with LIDAR methane measurements. Whether this is possible is not clear yet but will be asked for.
}\DIFdelend %DIF > This is an interesting study for us as well, especially if ESRANGE can support us with LIDAR methane measurements.
%DIF > Whether this is possible is not clear yet but will be asked for.

\DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{As a secondary goal we want to investigate, whether a correlation between different landscapes and methane levels in the stratosphere can be drawn. Depending on the quality of our data and number of data points we collected during the floating phase of the flight, we will study fluctuations and compare them with the GPS data collected by the balloon. Since freshwater bodies, wetlands and industrial areas have higher methane emissions than for example forests, we are interested whether methane fluctuations in the stratosphere can be related to those different landscapes. Machine learning might be employed to help recognising those fluctuations.
}\DIFdelend %DIF > As a secondary goal we want to investigate, whether a correlation between %different landscapes and methane levels in the stratosphere can be drawn.
%DIF > Depending on the quality of our data and number of data points we collected %during the floating phase of the flight, we will study fluctuations and %compare them with the GPS data collected by the balloon.
%DIF > Since freshwater bodies, wetlands and industrial areas have higher methane %emissions than for example forests, we are interested whether methane %fluctuations in the stratosphere can be related to those different landscapes.
%DIF > Machine learning might be employed to help recognising those fluctuations.

%DIF > %%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
\section{Launch Campaign}
\DIFaddbegin 

\DIFaddend This will be filled out in later versions of the \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{\mbox{%DIFAUXCMD
\ac{sed}}\hskip0pt%DIFAUXCMD
}\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{\mbox{%DIFAUXCMD
\gls{sed}}\hskip0pt%DIFAUXCMD
}\DIFaddend .

%DIF > %%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
\section{Results}

It is expected that \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{we will receive }\DIFdelend less data points with a quality lower than other in-situ missions \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{will be received by MIRAGE}\DIFaddend . However, if the calibration was done with the intended thoroughness, \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{we expect that }\DIFdelend the results should be comparable and correspond well to \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{\mbox{%DIFAUXCMD
\ac{rtm}}\hskip0pt%DIFAUXCMD
. We expect to get good insights into current }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{\mbox{%DIFAUXCMD
\gls{rtm}}\hskip0pt%DIFAUXCMD
. In that case, MIRAGE would have demonstrated that NDIR technology can measure }\DIFaddend methane levels in the stratosphere and \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{hope to }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{hopefully }\DIFaddend inspire the further development and application of \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{\mbox{%DIFAUXCMD
\ac{ndir} }\hskip0pt%DIFAUXCMD
}\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{\mbox{%DIFAUXCMD
\gls{ndir} }\hskip0pt%DIFAUXCMD
}\DIFaddend sensors for atmospheric monitoring.

\DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{There is a small chance that fluctuations of methane in the stratosphere can be connected to the emissions at ground level even if very precise measurement techniques are given. Especially, if we fly over different large areas like wetlands or lakes and forests, we might be able to detect changes in concentrations. Even if this goal fails, we will have collected methane concentration data with higher accuracy at about 25-30 km height due to the longer measurement time.
}\DIFdelend %DIF > There is a small chance that fluctuations of methane in the stratosphere can %be connected to the emissions at ground level even if very precise %measurement techniques are given. Especially, if we fly over different large %areas like wetlands or lakes and forests, we might be able to detect changes %in concentrations. Even if this goal fails, we will have collected methane %concentration data with higher accuracy at about 25-30 km height due to the %longer measurement time.

%DIF > %%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
\section{Input to Campaign Report}
\DIFaddbegin 

\DIFaddend This section will be filled after the Campaign.

%DIF > %%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
\section{Lessons Learned}
\DIFaddbegin 

\DIFaddend As a fresh and still growing team there were some lessons \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{we could take away so far}\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{learned already}\DIFaddend . 
\begin{enumerate}
  \item \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{We }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{It }\DIFaddend took too much time \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{to }\DIFdelend recruit new team members and \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{still miss }\DIFdelend key team members \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{are still missing; }\DIFaddend for example in software design.
  \item \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{We found out that we made wrong }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{Wrong }\DIFaddend assumptions regarding the accuracy of the K96 sensor \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{were made early one}\DIFaddend . This should have been prevented by asking more questions earlier on and closer communication with our contacts from Senseair \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{AB}\DIFaddend .
  \item \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{We need to take holidays into consideration and make }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{Holidays and }\DIFaddend more generous time estimates \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{to }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{need to be taken into consideration to }\DIFaddend prevent finishing crucial project steps like the \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{\mbox{%DIFAUXCMD
\ac{sed} }\hskip0pt%DIFAUXCMD
}\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{\mbox{%DIFAUXCMD
\gls{sed} }\hskip0pt%DIFAUXCMD
}\DIFaddend under pressure, which can lead to worse quality.
  \item \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{We have }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{It it important }\DIFaddend to keep track of progress and check in with each sub team or team members working on specific tasks to prevent delays.
\end{enumerate}
Of course there are many more lessons of more practical nature. Everyone knows a little more about pumps and their limitations now, \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{all of us }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{everyone }\DIFaddend learned something about methane sensing and many more things like that. However, the biggest lesson is probably that the documentation for such a project takes a lot of time and thought but also makes you aware of many aspects that can be easily overlooked.

\DIFaddbegin \label{app:checklists}

\chapter{\DIFadd{PLANNING}}\newpage

\chapter{\DIFadd{MATERIAL SAFETY DATA SHEETS}}\newpage 

\chapter{\DIFadd{THERMAL ANALYSIS BACKGROUND}}
\label{app:thermal_background}
\DIFadd{Assuming energy balance
}\begin{align}
    \DIFadd{\dot{E}_{\mathrm{system}}=\dot{E}_{\mathrm{in}}-\dot{E}_{out}\qc
}\end{align}
\DIFadd{the first law of thermodynamics 
}\begin{align}
    \DIFadd{\Delta E_\mathrm{system}=Q-W\qc
}\end{align}
\DIFadd{can be used to relate heat fluxes in and out of a system to each other. Here, $Q$ is the net heat added to the system and $W$ is the work done on the system. Assuming that no work is being done (no volume expansion), and that only the internal energy (as opposed to e.g. kinetic energy) is of interest, this becomes
}\begin{align}
    \DIFadd{\Delta }{\DIFadd{E}}\DIFadd{_\mathrm{system}}&\DIFadd{=Q}\\ 
    &\DIFadd{=m_s\cdot c_{p,s} \cdot \Delta }{\DIFadd{T}}\DIFadd{_s\qc
}\end{align}
\DIFadd{where the subscript $_s$ signals system parameters, $m$ is the mass, $c_p$ the specific heat capacity and $T$ the temperature. Finally, this gives 
}\begin{align}
    \DIFadd{m_s\cdot c_{p,s} \cdot \dot{T}_s }&\DIFadd{= \dot{E}_{\mathrm{in}}-\dot{E}_{out}}\\ 
    &\DIFadd{= \dot{Q}_\mathrm{in}-\dot{Q}_\mathrm{out}
}\end{align}
\DIFadd{For simplicity, in the following the $\dot{}$ is dropped from $Q$. 
}

\DIFadd{Potential contributors to the heat flux into the system $Q_\mathrm{in}$ are 
}\begin{itemize}
    \item \DIFadd{heat generated by electronics 
    }\item \DIFadd{heat generated by pumps 
    }\item \DIFadd{heat generated by pressurisation of air 
    }\item \DIFadd{heat through absorbance of solar radiation,
}\end{itemize}
\DIFadd{while contributors to heat flux out of the system $Q_\mathrm{out}$ are 
}\begin{itemize}
    \item \DIFadd{convective cooling via ambient air
    }\item \DIFadd{radiative cooling via emittance of infrared radiation.
}\end{itemize}
\DIFadd{In the following, a derivation of the individual contributions will follow. 
}

\paragraph{\DIFadd{The heat generated by compressed air}} \DIFadd{must be analysed in two stages, as there are two compression stages in the system. The first is facilitated by the vacuum pumps, pressurising ambient air to 1 bar, the second by the compressor, pressurising to 3 bar. The discharge temperature is defined as 
}\begin{align}
    \DIFadd{T_\mathrm{out}}&\DIFadd{=T_\mathrm{in}\mbox{%DIFAUXCMD
\qty}\hskip0pt%DIFAUXCMD
(\frac{p_\mathrm{out}}{p_\mathrm{in}})^{\frac{n-1}{n}}\qc
}\end{align}
\DIFadd{where $T_\mathrm{out}$ is the discharge temperature (air temperature leaving the pumps), $T_\mathrm{in}$ is the temperature of the air on the suction side, $p_\mathrm{in}$ is the air pressure on the suction side and $p_\mathrm{out}$ is the discharge pressure. $n$ is the polytropic factor, determined by the pump and gas. If the process were isentropic, the factor would be equal to the isentropic factor of air $\gamma=1.4$. For the diaphragm pump, due to negligible friction, $n<\gamma$ as the air is dumping part of its heat into the pump.
Conversely, the compressor, as a piston pump, might generate significant heat, further heating the air, such that $n>\gamma$.
Although the discharge temperature can reach up to 500°C (assuming $n=\gamma$), the actual heat capacity of that air diminishes, as the air density decreases. Thus the total heat energy carried by the air can be calculated as  
}\begin{align}
    \DIFadd{Q_\mathrm{heat} }&\DIFadd{= \dot{m}c_p\Delta T}\\ 
    &\DIFadd{= \rho I_\mathrm{pump} c_p\Delta T\qc
}\end{align}
\DIFadd{where $\rho$ is the air density, $I_{pump}$ the volumetric pump flow rate, $c_p$ the specific heat of air, and $\Delta T$ the temperature difference between suction side and discharge side. To determine the final discharge temperature, it needs to be calculated for the diaphragm pumps and based on that for the compressor.
}

\paragraph{\DIFadd{The heat generated by the pumps}} \DIFadd{themselves can be estimated from their power requirements and an efficiency factor. The diaphragm pumps will probably have a higher effiency than the piston compressor pump, since they generate less friction. The total heat is then estimated as 
}\begin{align}
    \DIFadd{Q_\mathrm{heat} }&\DIFadd{= \epsilon P_\mathrm{draw}\qc
}\end{align}
\DIFadd{where $\epsilon$ is the effiency factor ranging between 0 and 1 and $P_\mathrm{draw}$ is the electrical power consumption of the pump. Note, that the operations schedule implies $P_\mathrm{draw}$ to vary with time.
}

\paragraph{\DIFadd{The heat generated by other electric components}} \DIFadd{is similarly calculated. Given in \tref{tab:power_consumption} is the total power draw from the BEXUS system, as well as the total consumption. This is already assuming an effiency factor, such that the difference between those values is assumed to be emitted as heat. Thus,
}\begin{align}
     \DIFadd{Q_\mathrm{heat} }&\DIFadd{= P_\mathrm{draw}- P_\mathrm{consumed}   
}\end{align}

\paragraph{\DIFadd{The heat absorbed through solar radiation}} \DIFadd{depends on the system material, surface, and dimensions, as well as on weather conditions like cloudiness and time/solar zenith angle and altitude. In general the total heat flux is comprised of three components: direct flux from the sun, secondary solar flux reflected from Earth, clouds, and atmosphere and the heat of the earth in the \mbox{%DIFAUXCMD
\gls{ir}}\hskip0pt%DIFAUXCMD
. Thus,
}\begin{align}
    \DIFadd{q }&\DIFadd{= q_\mathrm{sun}+q_\mathrm{reflected}+q_\mathrm{IR\,earth}}\\
    &\DIFadd{=\alpha_s (I_s \cos{\theta}+ I_s a F_{se} ) + \alpha_\mathrm{IR}(q_\mathrm{earth} F_{se})\qc
}\end{align}
\DIFadd{where $\alpha_s$ is the solar absorptivity of the surface, $I_s$ is the solar intensity received, $\theta$ is the angle between surface normal and sun, $a$ is the albedo coefficient of the Earth and atmosphere, $F_{se}$ is the view factor relating the amount of surface viewing in direction of the Earth, $\alpha_\mathrm{IR}$ is the \mbox{%DIFAUXCMD
\gls{ir} }\hskip0pt%DIFAUXCMD
absorptivity of the surface, and $q_\mathrm{earth}$ is the \mbox{%DIFAUXCMD
\gls{ir} }\hskip0pt%DIFAUXCMD
heat flux emitted by the Earth. Most of these values are or can be assumed to be roughly constant with respect to height. As the chassis and chamber are made out of aluminum the following analysis assumes $\alpha_{IR}=\dos{\epsilon}=0.1$ and $\alpha_s=0.4$.
The most dramatic change with altitude is seen in $I_s$.}\par 
\DIFadd{The atmosphere is absorbing part of the solar radiation incident on the top atmospheric layers $G_{sc}$. This can be modeled via the Lambert-Beer law as 
}\begin{align}
    \DIFadd{I_s(h) = G_{sc}\tau_0^{\frac{p(h)}{p_0}\frac{1}{\cos{\phi}+  0.505072 * (96.07995 - \phi) ^ {-1.6364}
}}\qc
}\end{align}
\DIFadd{where $\tau_0$ is the transmittance of solar light at ground level, $p_0$ is the atmospheric pressure at ground level, $p(h)$ is the pressure at a given altitude, and $\phi$ is the zenith angle. This formula assumes zero clouds. Since the minimum expected zenith angle in Kiruna in October is 77.11°, this value will be used by the following analysis.}\par 
\DIFadd{The final heat load is determined by multiplying the heat load for individual surfaces and summing over them, i.e.
}\begin{align}
    \DIFadd{Q_\mathrm{heat}=\sum_i q_i A_i.
}\end{align}

\paragraph{\DIFadd{The heat lost via convective cooling}} \DIFadd{via ambient air depends on the ambient air density and on system geometry. In general, 
}\begin{align}
    \DIFadd{Q_\mathrm{heat,A} = hA(T_\mathrm{system}-T_\mathrm{ambient})\qc
}\end{align}
\DIFadd{where $A$ is the area of the respective surface, $h$ is the convection coefficient and $T_\mathrm{ambient}$ is the temperature of the ambient air. The convection coefficient depends on the air density and can be modelled as 
}\begin{align}
    \DIFadd{h = h_0 \mbox{%DIFAUXCMD
\qty}\hskip0pt%DIFAUXCMD
(\frac{p}{p_0})^n\qc
}\end{align}
\DIFadd{where $h_0$ defines some reference convection coefficient on the ground and is usually $h_0=15\,\mathrm{W/m^2/K}$. 
$n$ is usually set to 0.5 for balloon applications with laminar flow (true during ascend and floating phase, if the wind speed is not extremely high). The total heat load is again defined by adding the individual heat loads of the surfaces together.
}

\paragraph{\DIFadd{The heat lost via blackbody radiation}} \DIFadd{is defined via the Stefan-Boltzmann law 
}\begin{align}
    \DIFadd{Q_\mathrm{heat,A}}&\DIFadd{= A\varepsilon\sigma (T_\mathrm{system}^4-T_\mathrm{ambient}^4)\qc
}\end{align}
\DIFadd{where $\varepsilon$ is the emissivity and $\sigma$ is the Stefan-Boltzmann constant. The total heat load is again defined by adding the individual heat loads of the surfaces together.
}

\paragraph{\DIFadd{The lumped system approach}} \DIFadd{is used to model subsystems with thermally distinct behaviours. Here, this means that the system is split thermally into two parts: 
}\begin{itemize}
    \item \DIFadd{the box/chassis containing the electronics and components, 
    }\item \DIFadd{the pressure chamber within,
    }\item \DIFadd{and potentially isolation if considered (see cold and hot case in \sref{sec: thermal cold case} and \sref{sec: thermal hot case}, respectively).
}\end{itemize}
\DIFadd{The division of the system into the lumps chassis and chamber is done as the pressure chamber is to be kept at a different temperature than the rest of the system. First, the temperature of the compartment/chassis containing all electronics and components is calculated based on a constant temperature of the pressure chamber. Then, the heat flux necessary to keep the pressure chamber at the desired temperature is determined. This means that the heat flux between chamber and chassis is added as a heat load to both. 
This transfer flux is defined as 
}\begin{align}
    \DIFadd{Q_\mathrm{transfer} = F_g (T_\mathrm{chamber}-T_\mathrm{chassis}) + \epsilon_\mathrm{chamber}\sigma A_\mathrm{chamber}\mbox{%DIFAUXCMD
\qty}\hskip0pt%DIFAUXCMD
(T_\mathrm{chamber}^4-T_\mathrm{chassis}^4)
}\end{align}
\DIFadd{Here, $F_g$ is a coefficient determining the efficiency of conductive and radiative heat transfer between chassis and chamber. While this coefficient depends on the exact geometry and material of conncecting bolts and surfaces, as well as on the air density inside the chassis, it is approximated at $F_g\approx 1.5$ W/K. The second term is responsible for modeling the radiative heat transfer between chamber and chassis. As the chassis has a much greater surface area than the chamber, it is modelled as a heat sink. This means that the efficiency of the radiative transfer is solely determined by chamber emissivity $\epsilon$ and surface area $A$.
}

\paragraph{\DIFadd{Insulation}} \DIFadd{is modelled as a white styrofoam box of 5 cm thickness, completely encapsulating the chassis. The chassis is no longer subject to solar radiation, air convection, and can no longer loose heat via blackbody radiation. Thus, $Q_\mathrm{rad\,in}=Q_\mathrm{rad\,out}=Q_\mathrm{conv}=0$. Instead, the chassis is interacting with the styrofoam box via 
}\begin{align}
    \DIFadd{Q_\mathrm{insulation}=\kappa_\mathrm{styro}\frac{A_\mathrm{styro}}{d_\mathrm{styro}}\mbox{%DIFAUXCMD
\qty}\hskip0pt%DIFAUXCMD
(T_\mathrm{styro}-T_\mathrm{chassis})\qc
}\end{align} 
\DIFadd{where $\kappa_\mathrm{styro}$ is the thermal conductivity and set to be 0.033 $\mathrm{W\,m^{-1}\,K^{-1}}$, $A_\mathrm{styro}$ is the insulation surface area, $d_\mathrm{styro}$ its thickness and $T_\mathrm{styro}$ its outer temperature. The outer temperature in turn is defined via 
}\begin{align}
    \DIFadd{Q_\mathrm{rad\,in} = Q_\mathrm{rad\,out}+Q_\mathrm{conv} + Q_\mathrm{insulation}.
}\end{align}
\DIFadd{This means that a steady-state temperature of the styrofoam is assumed. This holds true if the styrofoam's thermal capacity is small compared to that of the chassis. Since styrofoam is lightweight and the chassis is made out of aluminium this is justified.
The \mbox{%DIFAUXCMD
\gls{ir} }\hskip0pt%DIFAUXCMD
emissivity is set to $\epsilon=\alpha_\mathrm{IR}=0.6$ and the solar absorptivity to $\alpha_s=0.15$.}\par 

\DIFadd{All in all this means that for the steady state assumptions used in the cold and hot cases, the following two equations need to be solved:
}\begin{align}
    \DIFadd{Q_\mathrm{ele}+Q_\mathrm{pump}+Q_\mathrm{transfer} + Q_\mathrm{rad\,in} + Q_\mathrm{insulation} }&\DIFadd{= Q_\mathrm{conv} + Q_\mathrm{rad\,out} \label{eq:heat_balance_compartment}}\\ 
    \DIFadd{Q_\mathrm{thermal\,system}+Q_\mathrm{compressed\,air} }&\DIFadd{=  Q_\mathrm{transfer} \label{eq:heat_balance_chamber}
}\end{align} 
\DIFadd{\eqref{eq:heat_balance_compartment} describes the heat balance of the bigger chassis and \eqref{eq:heat_balance_chamber} describes the heat balance of the pressure chamber within. Note, that depending on the assumptions in the cold and hot cases, some of the terms might be 0.%DIF > %%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
}\DIFaddend
